</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Klinkerfues" title="Wilhelm Klinkerfues">Wilhelm Klinkerfues</a>
</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Other notable students</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">

</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influences</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influenced</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Minding" title="Ferdinand Minding">Ferdinand Minding</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Signature</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg"><img alt="Carl Friedrich Gauß signature.svg" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="847" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg/150px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg/225px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg/300px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_signature.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Johann Carl Friedrich Gauss</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Gauß</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[kaʁl ˈfʁiːdʁɪç ˈɡaʊs]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg" title="De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Carolus Fridericus Gauss</i>; 30 April 1777 – 23 February 1855) was a German <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a> and physicist who made significant contributions to many fields in mathematics and science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Sometimes referred to as the <i>Princeps mathematicorum</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> for '"the foremost of mathematicians"') and "the greatest mathematician since antiquity", Gauss had an exceptional influence in many fields of mathematics and science, and is ranked among history's most influential mathematicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientificmonthly_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-scientificmonthly-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg/220px-Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg/330px-Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg/440px-Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue-of-Gauss-in-Braunschweig.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Gauss at his birthplace, <a href="/wiki/Braunschweig" title="Braunschweig">Brunswick</a></div></div></div>
<p>Johann Carl Friedrich Gauss was born on 30 April 1777 in <a href="/wiki/Braunschweig" title="Braunschweig">Brunswick (Braunschweig)</a>, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brunswick-Wolfenb%C3%BCttel" title="Duchy of Brunswick-Wolfenbüttel">Duchy of Brunswick-Wolfenbüttel</a> (now part of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a>, Germany), to poor, working-class parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wichita_state_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-wichita_state-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECayley1911_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECayley1911-7">[7]</a></sup> His mother was illiterate and never recorded the date of his birth, remembering only that he had been born on a Wednesday, eight days before the <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">Feast of the Ascension</a> (which occurs 39 days after Easter). Gauss later solved this puzzle about his birthdate in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">finding the date of Easter</a>, deriving methods to compute the date in both past and future years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> He was christened and <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmed</a> in a church near the school he attended as a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gauss was a <a href="/wiki/Child_prodigy" title="Child prodigy">child prodigy</a>. In his memorial on Gauss, <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Sartorius_von_Waltershausen" title="Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen">Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen</a> wrote that when Gauss was barely three years old he corrected a math error his father made; and that when he was seven, solved an <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_progression" title="Arithmetic progression">arithmetic series</a> problem faster than anyone else in his class of 100 pupils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> There are many versions of this story, with various details regarding the nature of the series – the most frequent being the classical problem of adding together all the integers from 1 to 100. (See also under "Anecdotes" below.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage178_178]_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage178_178]-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayesreckoning_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-hayesreckoning-13">[13]</a></sup> There are many other anecdotes about his precocity while a toddler, and he made his first groundbreaking mathematical discoveries while still a teenager. He completed his <a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">magnum opus</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae" title="Disquisitiones Arithmeticae">Disquisitiones Arithmeticae</a></i>, in 1798, at the age of 21, and it was published in 1801.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]-14">[14]</a></sup> This work was fundamental in consolidating number theory as a discipline and has shaped the field to the present day.
</p><p>Gauss's intellectual abilities attracted the attention of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_William_Ferdinand,_Duke_of_Brunswick" title="Charles William Ferdinand, Duke of Brunswick">Duke of Brunswick</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage178_178]_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage178_178]-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientificmonthly_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-scientificmonthly-5">[5]</a></sup> who sent him to the Collegium Carolinum (now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Braunschweig_University_of_Technology" title="Braunschweig University of Technology">Braunschweig University of Technology</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]-14">[14]</a></sup> which he attended from 1792 to 1795,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage178_178–9.]_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage178_178–9.]-15">[15]</a></sup> and to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen" title="University of Göttingen">University of Göttingen</a> from 1795 to 1798.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]-14">[14]</a></sup> While at university, Gauss independently rediscovered several important theorems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> His breakthrough occurred in 1796 when he showed that a regular <a href="/wiki/Polygon" title="Polygon">polygon</a> can be constructed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">compass and straightedge</a> if the number of its sides is the product of distinct <a href="/wiki/Fermat_number" title="Fermat number">Fermat primes</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation" title="Exponentiation">power</a> of 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[a]</a></sup> This was a major discovery in an important field of mathematics; construction problems had occupied mathematicians since the days of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greeks</a>, and the discovery ultimately led Gauss to choose mathematics instead of <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a> as a career.
Gauss was so pleased with this result that he requested that a regular <a href="/wiki/Heptadecagon" title="Heptadecagon">heptadecagon</a> be inscribed on his tombstone. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_masonry" title="Stone masonry">stonemason</a> declined, stating that the difficult construction would essentially look like a circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The year 1796 was productive for both Gauss and number theory. He discovered a construction of the heptadecagon on 30 March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> He further advanced <a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">modular arithmetic</a>, greatly simplifying manipulations in number theory. On 8 April he became the first to prove the <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity" title="Quadratic reciprocity">quadratic reciprocity</a> law. This remarkably general law allows mathematicians to determine the solvability of any quadratic equation in modular arithmetic. The <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a>, conjectured on 31 May, gives a good understanding of how the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> are distributed among the integers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eureka_Gauss.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="138" data-file-width="773" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Eureka_Gauss.png/220px-Eureka_Gauss.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Eureka_Gauss.png/330px-Eureka_Gauss.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Eureka_Gauss.png/440px-Eureka_Gauss.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eureka_Gauss.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_diary" title="Gauss's diary">Gauss's diary</a> entry related to sum of triangular numbers (1796)</div></div></div>
<p>Gauss also discovered that every positive integer is representable as a sum of at most three <a href="/wiki/Triangular_number" title="Triangular number">triangular numbers</a> on 10 July and then jotted down in <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_diary" title="Gauss's diary">his diary</a> the note: "<a href="/wiki/Eureka_(word)" title="Eureka (word)">ΕΥΡΗΚΑ</a>! <span class="nowrap">num = Δ + Δ + Δ</span>". On 1 October he published a result on the number of solutions of polynomials with coefficients in <a href="/wiki/Finite_field" title="Finite field">finite fields</a>, which 150 years later led to the <a href="/wiki/Weil_conjectures" title="Weil conjectures">Weil conjectures</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years_and_death">Later years and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Later years and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gauss remained mentally active into his old age, even while having <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a> and suffering general unhappiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181mode2up_181]_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181mode2up_181]-20">[19]</a></sup> For example, at the age of 62, he taught himself <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181mode2up_181]_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181mode2up_181]-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1840, Gauss published his influential <i>Dioptrische Untersuchungen</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981144–145_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981144–145-21">[20]</a></sup> in which he gave the first systematic analysis on the formation of images under a <a href="/wiki/Paraxial_approximation" title="Paraxial approximation">paraxial approximation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gaussian_optics" title="Gaussian optics">Gaussian optics</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hecht_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hecht-22">[21]</a></sup> Among his results, Gauss showed that under a paraxial approximation an optical system can be characterized by its <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_point_(optics)" title="Cardinal point (optics)">cardinal points</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bass_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bass-23">[22]</a></sup> and he derived the Gaussian lens formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostdiek_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostdiek-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1845, he became an associated member of the Royal Institute of the Netherlands; when that became the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences">Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1851, he joined as a foreign member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1854, Gauss selected the topic for <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a>'s inaugural lecture "Über die Hypothesen, welche der Geometrie zu Grunde liegen" (<i>About the hypotheses that underlie Geometry</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monastyrsky_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monastyrsky-26">[25]</a></sup> On the way home from Riemann's lecture, Weber reported that Gauss was full of praise and excitement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981154_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981154-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was elected as a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> in 1853.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed,_1855.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="538" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg/220px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg/330px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg/440px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed,_1855.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gauss on his deathbed (1855)</div></div></div>
<p>On 23 February 1855, Gauss died of a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a> in Göttingen (then <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Kingdom of Hanover</a> and now <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wichita_state_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-wichita_state-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181_181]_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181_181]-29">[28]</a></sup> he is interred in the <a href="/wiki/Albanifriedhof" title="Albanifriedhof">Albani Cemetery</a> there. Two people gave eulogies at his funeral: Gauss's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Ewald" title="Heinrich Ewald">Heinrich Ewald</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Sartorius_von_Waltershausen" title="Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen">Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen</a>, who was Gauss's close friend and biographer. Gauss's brain was preserved and was studied by <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Wagner" title="Rudolf Wagner">Rudolf Wagner</a>, who found its mass to be slightly above average, at 1,492 grams (52.6 oz), and the cerebral area equal to 219,588 square millimetres (340.362 in<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sqmm_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-sqmm-30">[b]</a></sup> Highly developed convolutions were also found, which in the early 20th century were suggested as the explanation of his genius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bardi_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-bardi-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gauss was nominally a member of the St. Albans Evangelical Lutheran church in Göttingen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300-32">[30]</a></sup> One of his biographers, <a href="/wiki/G._Waldo_Dunnington" title="G. Waldo Dunnington">G. Waldo Dunnington</a>, has described Gauss's religious views as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For him science was the means of exposing the immortal nucleus of the human soul. In the days of his full strength, it furnished him recreation and, by the prospects which it opened up to him, gave consolation. Toward the end of his life, it brought him confidence. Gauss's God was not a cold and distant figment of metaphysics, nor a distorted caricature of embittered theology. To man is not vouchsafed that fullness of knowledge which would warrant his arrogantly holding that his blurred vision is the full light and that there can be none other which might report the truth as does his. For Gauss, not he who mumbles his creed, but he who lives it, is accepted. He believed that a life worthily spent here on earth is the best, the only, preparation for heaven. Religion is not a question of literature, but of life. God's revelation is continuous, not contained in tablets of stone or sacred parchment. A book is inspired when it inspires. The unshakeable idea of personal continuance after death, the firm belief in a last regulator of things, in an eternal, just, omniscient, omnipotent God, formed the basis of his religious life, which harmonized completely with his scientific research.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, pp. 298–301</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Apart from his correspondence, there are not many known details about Gauss's personal creed. Many biographers of Gauss disagree about his religious stance, with Bühler and others considering him a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a> with very unorthodox views,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerhard_Falk_1995_121_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerhard_Falk_1995_121-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981152_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981152-35">[33]</a></sup> while Dunnington (admitting that Gauss did not believe literally in all Christian dogmas and that it is unknown what he believed on most doctrinal and confessional questions) points out that he was, at least, a nominal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[c]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3063" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg/220px-Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg/330px-Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg/440px-Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F_at_Albani-Friedhof_G%C3%B6ttingen_2017_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gauss's gravesite at <a href="/wiki/Albanifriedhof" title="Albanifriedhof">Albani Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="Göttingen">Göttingen</a>, Germany</div></div></div>
<p>In connection to this, there is a record of a conversation between <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Wagner" title="Rudolf Wagner">Rudolf Wagner</a> and Gauss, in which they discussed <a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a>'s book <i>Of the Plurality of Worlds</i>. In this work, Whewell had discarded the possibility of existing life in other planets, on the basis of theological arguments, but this was a position with which both Wagner and Gauss disagreed. Later Wagner explained that he did not fully believe in the Bible, though he confessed that he "envied" those who were able to easily believe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[d]</a></sup> This later led them to discuss the topic of <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>, and in some other religious remarks, Gauss said that he had been more influenced by theologians like Lutheran minister <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gerhardt" title="Paul Gerhardt">Paul Gerhardt</a> than by <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004356_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004356-38">[34]</a></sup> Other religious influences included Wilhelm Braubach, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Peter_S%C3%BCssmilch" title="Johann Peter Süssmilch">Johann Peter Süssmilch</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>. Two religious works which Gauss read frequently were Braubach's <i>Seelenlehre</i> (Giessen, 1843) and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Peter_S%C3%BCssmilch" title="Johann Peter Süssmilch">Süssmilch</a>'s <i>Gottliche</i> (Ordnung gerettet, 1756); he also devoted considerable time to the New Testament in the original Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004305_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004305-39">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Dunnington further elaborates on Gauss's religious views by writing: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Gauss's religious consciousness was based on an insatiable thirst for truth and a deep feeling of justice extending to intellectual as well as material goods. He conceived spiritual life in the whole universe as a great system of law penetrated by eternal truth, and from this source he gained the firm confidence that death does not end all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300-32">[30]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Gauss believed in an omniscient source of creation however he claimed that belief or a lack of it did not affect his mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though he was not a church-goer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup> Gauss strongly upheld <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a>, believing "that one is not justified in disturbing another's religious belief, in which they find consolation for earthly sorrows in time of trouble."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientificmonthly_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-scientificmonthly-5">[5]</a></sup> When his son Eugene announced that he wanted to become a Christian missionary, Gauss approved of this, saying that regardless of the problems within religious organizations, missionary work was "a highly honorable" task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004311_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004311-42">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Therese_Gauss.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="280" decoding="async" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Therese_Gauss.jpg/220px-Therese_Gauss.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/Therese_Gauss.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Therese_Gauss.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gauss's daughter Therese (1816–1864)</div></div></div>
<p>On 9 October 1805,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup> Gauss married Johanna Osthoff (1780–1809), and had two sons and a daughter with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]-44">[40]</a></sup> Johanna died on 11 October 1809,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]-44">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> and her youngest child, Louis, died the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup> Gauss plunged into a depression from which he never fully recovered. He then married Minna Waldeck (1788–1831)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]-44">[40]</a></sup> on 4 August 1810,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup> and had three more children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]-44">[40]</a></sup> Gauss was never quite the same without his first wife, and just like his father, grew to dominate his children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]-44">[40]</a></sup> Minna Waldeck died on 12 September 1831.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_43-6"><a href="#cite_note-:2-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage180_180]-44">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gauss had six children. With Johanna (1780–1809), his children were Joseph (1806–1873), Wilhelmina (1808–1846) and Louis (1809–1810). With Minna Waldeck he also had three children: Eugene (1811–1896), Wilhelm (1813–1879) and Therese (1816–1864). Eugene shared a good measure of Gauss's talent in languages and computation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gausschildren_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-gausschildren-46">[42]</a></sup> After his second wife's death in 1831 Therese took over the household and cared for Gauss for the rest of his life. His mother lived in his house from 1817 until her death in 1839.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientificmonthly_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-scientificmonthly-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gauss eventually had conflicts with his sons. He did not want any of his sons to enter mathematics or science for "fear of lowering the family name", as he believed none of them would surpass his own achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gausschildren_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-gausschildren-46">[42]</a></sup> Gauss wanted Eugene to become a lawyer, but Eugene wanted to study languages. They had an argument over a party Eugene held, for which Gauss refused to pay. The son left in anger and, in about 1832, emigrated to the United States. While working for the <a href="/wiki/American_Fur_Company" title="American Fur Company">American Fur Company</a> in the Midwest, he learned the Sioux language. Later, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> and became a successful businessman. Wilhelm also moved to America in 1837 and settled in Missouri, starting as a farmer and later becoming wealthy in the shoe business in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis</a>. It took many years for Eugene's success to counteract his reputation among Gauss's friends and colleagues. See also <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Robert_Gauss_to_Felix_Klein_-_September_3,_1912" title="s:Robert Gauss to Felix Klein - September 3, 1912">the letter from Robert Gauss to Felix Klein</a> on 3 September 1912.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gauss-16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4339" data-file-width="3247" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gauss-16.jpg/220px-Gauss-16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gauss-16.jpg/330px-Gauss-16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gauss-16.jpg/440px-Gauss-16.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gauss-16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Gauss in Volume II of "<i>Carl Friedrich Gauss Werke</i>," 1876</div></div></div>
<p>Gauss was an ardent <a href="/wiki/Perfectionism_(psychology)" title="Perfectionism (psychology)">perfectionist</a> and a hard worker. He was never a prolific writer, refusing to publish work which he did not consider complete and above criticism. This was in keeping with his personal motto <i>pauca sed matura</i> ("few, but ripe"). His personal diaries indicate that he had made several important mathematical discoveries years or decades before his contemporaries published them. Scottish-American mathematician and writer <a href="/wiki/Eric_Temple_Bell" title="Eric Temple Bell">Eric Temple Bell</a> said that if Gauss had published all of his discoveries in a timely manner, he would have advanced mathematics by fifty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though he did take in a few students, Gauss was known to dislike teaching. It is said that he attended only a single scientific conference, which was in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> in 1828. Several of his students became influential mathematicians, among them <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dedekind" title="Richard Dedekind">Richard Dedekind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a>.
</p><p>On Gauss's recommendation, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel" title="Friedrich Bessel">Friedrich Bessel</a> was awarded an honorary doctoral degree from Göttingen in March 1811.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bessel_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bessel-48">[e]</a></sup> Around that time, the two men engaged in a correspondence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> However, when they met in person in 1825, they quarrelled; the details are unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before she died, <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Germain" title="Sophie Germain">Sophie Germain</a> was recommended by Gauss to receive an honorary degree; she never received it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gauss usually declined to present the intuition behind his often very elegant proofs—he preferred them to appear "out of thin air" and erased all traces of how he discovered them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is justified, if unsatisfactorily, by Gauss in his <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae" title="Disquisitiones Arithmeticae">Disquisitiones Arithmeticae</a></i>, where he states that all analysis (i.e., the paths one traveled to reach the solution of a problem) must be suppressed for sake of brevity.
</p><p>Gauss supported the monarchy and opposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>, whom he saw as an outgrowth of revolution.
</p><p>Gauss summarized his views on the pursuit of knowledge in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Farkas_Bolyai" title="Farkas Bolyai">Farkas Bolyai</a> dated 2 September 1808 as follows:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is not knowledge, but the act of learning, not possession but the act of getting there, which grants the greatest enjoyment. When I have clarified and exhausted a subject, then I turn away from it, in order to go into darkness again. The never-satisfied man is so strange; if he has completed a structure, then it is not in order to dwell in it peacefully, but in order to begin another. I imagine the world conqueror must feel thus, who, after one kingdom is scarcely conquered, stretches out his arms for others.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, p. 416</cite></div></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_and_achievements">Career and achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Career and achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2012</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Disqvisitiones-800.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="478" decoding="async" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Disqvisitiones-800.jpg/220px-Disqvisitiones-800.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Disqvisitiones-800.jpg/330px-Disqvisitiones-800.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Disqvisitiones-800.jpg/440px-Disqvisitiones-800.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Disqvisitiones-800.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of Gauss's magnum opus, <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae" title="Disquisitiones Arithmeticae">Disquisitiones Arithmeticae</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>In his 1799 doctorate in absentia, <i>A new proof of the theorem that every integral rational algebraic function of one variable can be resolved into real factors of the first or second degree</i>, Gauss proved the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra" title="Fundamental theorem of algebra">fundamental theorem of algebra</a> which states that every non-constant single-variable <a href="/wiki/Polynomial" title="Polynomial">polynomial</a> with complex coefficients has at least one complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" title="Root of a function">root</a>. Mathematicians including <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d'Alembert">Jean le Rond d'Alembert</a> had produced false proofs before him, and Gauss's dissertation contains a critique of d'Alembert's work. Ironically, by today's standard, Gauss's own attempt is not acceptable, owing to the implicit use of the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_curve_theorem" title="Jordan curve theorem">Jordan curve theorem</a>. However, he subsequently produced three other proofs, the last one in 1849 being generally rigorous. His attempts clarified the concept of complex numbers considerably along the way.
</p><p>Gauss also made important contributions to <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> with his 1801 book <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae" title="Disquisitiones Arithmeticae">Disquisitiones Arithmeticae</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, Arithmetical Investigations), which, among other things, introduced the <a href="/wiki/Triple_bar" title="Triple bar">triple bar</a> symbol <span class="texhtml">≡</span> for <a href="/wiki/Congruence_relation" title="Congruence relation">congruence</a> and used it in a clean presentation of <a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">modular arithmetic</a>, contained the first two proofs of the law of <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity" title="Quadratic reciprocity">quadratic reciprocity</a>, developed the theories of binary and ternary <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_form" title="Quadratic form">quadratic forms</a>, stated the <a href="/wiki/Class_number_problem" title="Class number problem">class number problem</a> for them, and showed that a regular <a href="/wiki/Heptadecagon" title="Heptadecagon">heptadecagon</a> (17-sided polygon) can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">constructed with straightedge and compass</a>. It appears that Gauss already knew the <a href="/wiki/Class_number_formula" title="Class number formula">class number formula</a> in 1801.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, he proved the following conjectured theorems:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fermat_polygonal_number_theorem" title="Fermat polygonal number theorem">Fermat polygonal number theorem</a> for <i>n</i> = 3</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem" title="Fermat's Last Theorem">Fermat's Last Theorem</a> for <i>n</i> = 5</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes%27s_rule_of_signs" title="Descartes's rule of signs">Descartes's rule of signs</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kepler_conjecture" title="Kepler conjecture">Kepler conjecture</a> for regular arrangements</li></ul>
<p>He also
</p>
<ul><li>explained the <a href="/wiki/Pentagramma_mirificum" title="Pentagramma mirificum">pentagramma mirificum</a> (see <a class="external text" href="https://www.math.uni-bielefeld.de/~sek/cluster/pentagramma/" rel="nofollow">University of Bielefeld website</a>)</li>
<li>developed an algorithm for determining the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computus#Gauss'_Easter_algorithm" title="Computus">date of Easter</a></li>
<li>invented the <a href="/wiki/Cooley%E2%80%93Tukey_FFT_algorithm" title="Cooley–Tukey FFT algorithm">Cooley–Tukey FFT algorithm</a> for calculating the <a href="/wiki/Discrete_Fourier_transform" title="Discrete Fourier transform">discrete Fourier transforms</a> 160 years before Cooley and Tukey</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_1828.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="732" data-file-width="644" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg/220px-Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg/330px-Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg/440px-Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_1828.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Gauss published in <i><a href="/wiki/Astronomische_Nachrichten" title="Astronomische Nachrichten">Astronomische Nachrichten</a></i> (1828)</div></div></div>
<p>On 1 January 1801, Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Piazzi" title="Giuseppe Piazzi">Giuseppe Piazzi</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach2011[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbR9HAAAAQBAJpgPA470_470]_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach2011[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbR9HAAAAQBAJpgPA470_470]-53">[48]</a></sup> Piazzi could track Ceres for only somewhat more than a month, following it for three degrees across the night sky. Then it disappeared temporarily behind the glare of the Sun. Several months later, when Ceres should have reappeared, Piazzi could not locate it: the mathematical tools of the time were not able to extrapolate a position from such a scant amount of data—three degrees represent less than 1% of the total orbit. Gauss heard about the problem and tackled it. After three months of intense work, he predicted a position for Ceres in December 1801—just about a year after its first sighting—and this turned out to be accurate within a half-degree when it was rediscovered by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Xaver_von_Zach" title="Franz Xaver von Zach">Franz Xaver von Zach</a> on 31 December at <a href="/wiki/Gotha_Observatory" title="Gotha Observatory">Gotha</a>, and one day later by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Matth%C3%A4us_Olbers" title="Heinrich Wilhelm Matthäus Olbers">Heinrich Olbers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage179_179]-14">[14]</a></sup> This confirmation eventually led to the classification of Ceres as <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_designation" title="Minor-planet designation">minor-planet designation</a> 1 Ceres: the first <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> (now dwarf planet) ever discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_method" title="Gauss's method">Gauss's method</a> involved determining a <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic section</a> in space, given one focus (the Sun) and the conic's intersection with three given lines (lines of sight from the Earth, which is itself moving on an ellipse, to the planet) and given the time it takes the planet to traverse the arcs determined by these lines (from which the lengths of the arcs can be calculated by <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's Second Law</a>). This problem leads to an equation of the eighth degree, of which one solution, the Earth's orbit, is known. The solution sought is then separated from the remaining six based on physical conditions. In this work, Gauss used comprehensive approximation methods which he created for that purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>One such method was the <a href="/wiki/Fast_Fourier_transform" title="Fast Fourier transform">fast Fourier transform</a>. While this method is attributed to a 1965 paper by <a href="/wiki/James_Cooley" title="James Cooley">James Cooley</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Tukey" title="John Tukey">John Tukey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup> Gauss developed it as a trigonometric interpolation method. His paper, <i>Theoria Interpolationis Methodo Nova Tractata</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup> was published only posthumously in Volume 3 of his collected works. This paper predates the first presentation by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Fourier" title="Joseph Fourier">Joseph Fourier</a> on the subject in 1807.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zach noted that "without the intelligent work and calculations of Doctor Gauss we might not have found Ceres again". Though Gauss had up to that point been financially supported by his stipend from the Duke, he doubted the security of this arrangement, and also did not believe pure mathematics to be important enough to deserve support. Thus he sought a position in astronomy, and in 1807 was appointed Professor of Astronomy and Director of the astronomical <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Observatory" title="Göttingen Observatory">observatory in Göttingen</a>, a post he held for the remainder of his life.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg/220px-Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg/330px-Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg/440px-Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Normal_Distribution_PDF.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distributions</a></div></div></div>
<p>The discovery of Ceres led Gauss to his work on a theory of the motion of planetoids disturbed by large planets, eventually published in 1809 as <i>Theoria motus corporum coelestium in sectionibus conicis solem ambientum</i> (Theory of motion of the celestial bodies moving in conic sections around the Sun). In the process, he so streamlined the cumbersome mathematics of 18th-century orbital prediction that his work remains a cornerstone of astronomical computation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> It introduced the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_gravitational_constant" title="Gaussian gravitational constant">Gaussian gravitational constant</a>, and contained an influential treatment of the <a href="/wiki/Least_squares" title="Least squares">method of least squares</a>, a procedure used in all sciences to this day to minimize the impact of <a href="/wiki/Observational_error" title="Observational error">measurement error</a>.
</p><p>Gauss proved the method under the assumption of <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normally distributed</a> errors (see <a href="/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Markov_theorem" title="Gauss–Markov theorem">Gauss–Markov theorem</a>; see also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_topics_named_after_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="List of topics named after Carl Friedrich Gauss">Gaussian</a>). The method had been described earlier by <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre" title="Adrien-Marie Legendre">Adrien-Marie Legendre</a> in 1805, but Gauss claimed that he had been using it since 1794 or 1795.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[56]</a></sup> In the history of statistics, this disagreement is called the "priority dispute over the discovery of the method of least squares."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geodetic_survey">Geodetic survey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geodetic survey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg/220px-Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg/330px-Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg/440px-Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gau%C3%9F-Stein_Garlste.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Survey_marker" title="Survey marker">Survey marker</a> stone in Garlste (now Garlstedt)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1818 Gauss, putting his calculation skills to practical use, carried out a <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">geodetic survey</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Kingdom of Hanover</a> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gaussian_land_survey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gaussian land survey (page does not exist)">Gaussian land survey</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gau%C3%9Fsche_Landesaufnahme" title="de:Gaußsche Landesaufnahme">de</a>]</span>), linking up with previous Danish surveys. To aid the survey, Gauss invented the <a href="/wiki/Heliotrope_(instrument)" title="Heliotrope (instrument)">heliotrope</a>, an instrument that uses a mirror to reflect sunlight over great distances, to measure positions.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg/220px-10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg/330px-10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg/440px-10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:10_DM_Serie4_Rueckseite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Back of German 10-Deutsche Mark Banknote (1993; discontinued) featuring the <a href="/wiki/Heliotrope_(instrument)" title="Heliotrope (instrument)">heliotrope</a> and a section of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triangulation_network" title="Triangulation network">triangulation network</a> carried out by Gauss, in which this instrument was used.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1828, when studying differences in <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, Gauss first defined a physical approximation for the <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth" title="Figure of the Earth">figure of the Earth</a> as the surface everywhere perpendicular to the direction of gravity (of which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_sea_level" title="Mean sea level">mean sea level</a> makes up a part), later called the <i><a href="/wiki/Geoid" title="Geoid">geoid</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gauß1828_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gauß1828-63">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Euclidean_geometries">Non-Euclidean geometries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Non-Euclidean geometries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gauss also claimed to have discovered the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">non-Euclidean geometries</a> but never published it. This discovery was a major <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift" title="Paradigm shift">paradigm shift</a> in mathematics, as it freed mathematicians from the mistaken belief that Euclid's axioms were the only way to make geometry consistent and non-contradictory.
</p><p>Research on these geometries led to, among other things, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s theory of general relativity, which describes the universe as non-Euclidean. His friend <a href="/wiki/Farkas_Bolyai" title="Farkas Bolyai">Farkas Wolfgang Bolyai</a> with whom Gauss had sworn "brotherhood and the banner of truth" as a student, had tried in vain for many years to prove the parallel postulate from Euclid's other axioms of geometry.
</p><p>Bolyai's son, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Bolyai" title="János Bolyai">János Bolyai</a>, discovered non-Euclidean geometry in 1829; his work was published in 1832. After seeing it, Gauss wrote to Farkas Bolyai: "To praise it would amount to praising myself. For the entire content of the work ... coincides almost exactly with my own meditations which have occupied my mind for the past thirty or thirty-five years." This unproved statement put a strain on his relationship with Bolyai who thought that Gauss was stealing his idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krantz2010_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krantz2010-64">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Letters from Gauss years before 1829 reveal him obscurely discussing the problem of parallel lines. <a href="/wiki/G._Waldo_Dunnington" title="G. Waldo Dunnington">Waldo Dunnington</a>, a biographer of Gauss, argues in <i>Gauss, Titan of Science</i> (1955) that Gauss was in fact in full possession of non-Euclidean geometry long before it was published by Bolyai, but that he refused to publish any of it because of his fear of controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theorema_Egregium">Theorema Egregium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Theorema Egregium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The geodetic survey of Hanover, which required Gauss to spend summers traveling on horseback for a decade,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> fueled Gauss's interest in <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>, fields of mathematics dealing with <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surface_(topology)" title="Surface (topology)">surfaces</a>. Among other things, he came up with the notion of <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_curvature" title="Gaussian curvature">Gaussian curvature</a>.
This led in 1828 to an important theorem, the <a href="/wiki/Theorema_Egregium" title="Theorema Egregium">Theorema Egregium</a> (<i>remarkable theorem</i>), establishing an important property of the notion of <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a>. Informally, the theorem says that the curvature of a surface can be determined entirely by measuring <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distance" title="Distance">distances</a> on the surface.
</p><p>That is, curvature does not depend on how the surface might be <a href="/wiki/Embedding" title="Embedding">embedded</a> in 3-dimensional space or 2-dimensional space.
</p><p>In 1821, he was made a foreign member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a>. Gauss was elected a Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1822.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAS_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-68">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetism">Magnetism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Magnetism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
