</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Electromagnetism" title="Template:Electromagnetism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Electromagnetism" title="Template talk:Electromagnetism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Electromagnetism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 1831, Gauss developed a fruitful collaboration with the physics professor <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">Wilhelm Weber</a>, leading to new knowledge in <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> (including finding a representation for the unit of magnetism in terms of mass, charge, and time) and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_circuit_laws" title="Kirchhoff's circuit laws">Kirchhoff's circuit laws</a> in electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181_181]_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003[httpsarchiveorgdetailsmathmathematicia00brunpage181_181]-29">[28]</a></sup> It was during this time that he formulated his namesake <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law" title="Gauss's law">law</a>. They constructed the first <a href="/wiki/Electrical_telegraph" title="Electrical telegraph">electromechanical telegraph</a> in 1833,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruno2003181_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruno2003181-69">[64]</a></sup> which connected the observatory with the institute for physics in Göttingen. Gauss ordered a magnetic <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> to be built in the garden of the observatory, and with Weber founded the "Magnetischer Verein" (<i>magnetic association</i>), which supported measurements of Earth's magnetic field in many regions of the world. He developed a method of measuring the horizontal intensity of the magnetic field which was in use well into the second half of the 20th century, and worked out the mathematical theory for separating the inner and outer (<a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetospheric</a>) sources of Earth's magnetic field.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appraisal">Appraisal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Appraisal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The British mathematician <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Stephen_Smith" title="Henry John Stephen Smith">Henry John Stephen Smith</a> (1826–1883) gave the following appraisal of Gauss:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If we except the great name of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a> it is probable that no mathematicians of any age or country have ever surpassed Gauss in the combination of an abundant fertility of invention with an absolute rigorousness in demonstration, which the ancient Greeks themselves might have envied. It may seem paradoxical, but it is probably nevertheless true that it is precisely the efforts after logical perfection of form which has rendered the writings of Gauss open to the charge of obscurity and unnecessary difficulty. Gauss says more than once that, for brevity, he gives only the synthesis, and suppresses the analysis of his propositions. If, on the other hand, we turn to a memoir of <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Euler</a>'s, there is a sort of free and luxuriant gracefulness about the whole performance, which tells of the quiet pleasure which Euler must have taken in each step of his work. It is not the least of Gauss's claims to the admiration of mathematicians, that, while fully penetrated with a sense of the vastness of the science, he exacted the utmost rigorousness in every part of it, never passed over a difficulty, as if it did not exist, and never accepted a theorem as true beyond the limits within which it could actually be demonstrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anecdotes">Anecdotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Anecdotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are several stories of his early genius. According to one, his gifts became very apparent at the age of three when he corrected, mentally and without fault in his calculations, an error his father had made on paper while calculating finances.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Another story has it that in primary school after the young Gauss misbehaved, his teacher, J.G. Büttner, gave him a task: add a list of <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_progression" title="Arithmetic progression">arithmetic progression</a>; as the story is most often told, these were the numbers from 1 to 100. The young Gauss reputedly produced the correct answer within seconds, to the astonishment of his teacher and his assistant <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Martin_Bartels" title="Johann Christian Martin Bartels">Martin Bartels</a>. Gauss's presumed method was to realize that pairwise addition of terms from opposite ends of the list yielded identical intermediate sums: 1 + 100 = 101, 2 + 99 = 101, 3 + 98 = 101, and so on, for a <a href="/wiki/Triangular_number" title="Triangular number">total sum</a> of 50 × 101 = 5050.
However, the details of the story are at best uncertain (see<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayesreckoning_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-hayesreckoning-13">[13]</a></sup> for discussion of the original <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Sartorius_von_Waltershausen" title="Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen">Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen</a> source and the changes in other versions), and some authors, such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_J._Rotman" title="Joseph J. Rotman">Joseph J. Rotman</a> in his book <i>A First Course in Abstract Algebra</i> (2005), question whether it ever happened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>He referred to mathematics as "the queen of sciences"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup> and supposedly once espoused a belief in the necessity of immediately understanding <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_identity" title="Euler's identity">Euler's identity</a> as a benchmark pursuant to becoming a first-class mathematician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First-Class_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-First-Class-73">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemorations">Commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="List of things named after Carl Friedrich Gauss">List of things named after Carl Friedrich Gauss</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="1530" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg/220px-10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg/330px-10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg/440px-10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:10_DM_Serie4_Vorderseite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German 10-<a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">Deutsche Mark</a> <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">Banknote</a> (1993; discontinued) featuring Gauss</div></div></div>
<p>From 1989 through 2001, Gauss's portrait, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_distribution_curve" title="Normal distribution curve">normal distribution curve</a> and some prominent <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="Göttingen">Göttingen</a> buildings were featured on the German ten-mark banknote.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The reverse featured the approach for <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Hanover</a>. Germany has also issued three postage stamps honoring Gauss. One (no. 725) appeared in 1955 on the hundredth anniversary of his death; two others, nos. 1246 and 1811, in 1977, the 200th anniversary of his birth.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kehlmann" title="Daniel Kehlmann">Daniel Kehlmann</a>'s 2005 novel <i>Die Vermessung der Welt</i>, translated into English as <i><a href="/wiki/Measuring_the_World" title="Measuring the World">Measuring the World</a></i> (2006), explores Gauss's life and work through a lens of historical fiction, contrasting them with those of the German explorer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a>. A film version directed by <a href="/wiki/Detlev_Buck" title="Detlev Buck">Detlev Buck</a> was released in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007 a <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">bust</a> of Gauss was placed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walhalla_temple" title="Walhalla temple">Walhalla temple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="List of things named after Carl Friedrich Gauss">numerous things named in honor of Gauss</a> include:
</p>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a>, also known as the Gaussian distribution, the most common bell curve in statistics;</li>
<li>the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss_Prize" title="Gauss Prize">Gauss Prize</a>, one of the highest honors in mathematics;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gaussian_units" title="Gaussian units">Gaussian units</a>, the most common of the several electromagnetic unit systems based on  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre_gram_second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre gram second system of units">CGS units</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a>, CGS unit for <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>.</li></ul>
<p>In 1929 the Polish mathematician <a href="/wiki/Marian_Rejewski" title="Marian Rejewski">Marian Rejewski</a>, who helped to solve the German <a href="/wiki/Enigma_machine" title="Enigma machine">Enigma cipher machine</a> in December 1932, began studying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actuarial_statistics" title="Actuarial statistics">actuarial statistics</a> at <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="Göttingen">Göttingen</a>. At the request of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84_University" title="Poznań University">Poznań University</a> professor, <a href="/wiki/Zdzis%C5%82aw_Krygowski" title="Zdzisław Krygowski">Zdzisław Krygowski</a>, on arriving at Göttingen Rejewski laid flowers on Gauss's grave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 April 2018, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> honored Gauss on his would-be 241st birthday with a <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a> showcased in Europe, Russia, Israel, Japan, Taiwan, parts of Southern and Central America and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carl Friedrich Gauss, who also introduced the so-called <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_logarithm" title="Gaussian logarithm">Gaussian logarithms</a>, sometimes gets confused with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich_Gustav_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Friedrich Gustav Gauss (page does not exist)">Friedrich Gustav Gauss</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Gustav_Gau%C3%9F" title="de:Friedrich Gustav Gauß">de</a>]</span> (1829–1915), a German geologist, who also published some well-known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logarithm_table" title="Logarithm table">logarithm tables</a> used up into the early 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kühn_2008_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kühn_2008-78">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>1799: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctoral_dissertation" title="Doctoral dissertation">Doctoral dissertation</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra" title="Fundamental theorem of algebra">fundamental theorem of algebra</a>, with the title: <i>Demonstratio nova theorematis omnem functionem algebraicam rationalem integram unius variabilis in factores reales primi vel secundi gradus resolvi posse</i> ("New proof of the theorem that every integral algebraic function of one variable can be resolved into real factors (i.e., polynomials) of the first or second degree")</li>

<li>1832: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1">"Theoria residuorum biquadraticorum, Commentatio secunda". German translation by H. Maser <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><i>Untersuchungen über höhere Arithmetik (Disquisitiones Arithmeticae &amp; other papers on number theory)</i> (Second ed.). New York: Chelsea. 1965. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8284-0191-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8284-0191-3"><bdi>978-0-8284-0191-3</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Untersuchungen+%C3%BCber+h%C3%B6here+Arithmetik+%28Disquisitiones+Arithmeticae+%26+other+papers+on+number+theory%29&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.edition=Second&amp;rft.pub=Chelsea&amp;rft.date=1965&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8284-0191-3&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACarl+Friedrich+Gauss"></span>, pp. 534–586 [Introduces the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaussian_integers" title="Gaussian integers">Gaussian integers</a>, states (without proof) the law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biquadratic_reciprocity" title="Biquadratic reciprocity">biquadratic reciprocity</a>, proves the supplementary law for 1 + <i>i</i>]</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gauss stated without proof that this condition was also necessary, but never published his proof. A full proof of necessity was given by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Wantzel" title="Pierre Wantzel">Pierre Wantzel</a>. See the <a href="/wiki/Constructible_polygon" title="Constructible polygon">Constructible polygon</a> article for further discussion.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-sqmm-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-sqmm_30-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDonaldson1891">Donaldson 1891</a>, pp. 248–294 says: "Gauss, 1492 grm. 957 grm. 219588. sq. mm."; i.e. the unit is <i>square mm</i>. In the later reference: Dunnington (1927), the unit is erroneously reported as square cm, which gives an unreasonably large area; the 1891 reference is more reliable.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, p. 305 writes "It is not known just what Gauss believed on most doctrinal and confessional questions. He did not believe literally in all Christian dogmas. Officially he was a member of St. Albans Church (Evangelical Lutheran) in Gottingen. All baptisms, burials, and weddings in his family occurred there. It is also not known whether he attended church regularly or contributed financially. A faculty colleague called Gauss a deist, but there is good reason to believe that this label did not fit well. Gauss possessed strong religious tolerance which he carried over to every belief originating in the depths of the human heart. This tolerance is not to be confused with religious indifference. He took a special interest in the religious development of the human race, especially in his own century. With reference to the manifold denominations, which frequently did not agree with his views, he always emphasized that one is not justified in disturbing the faith of others in which they find consolation for earthly sufferings and a safe refuge in days of misfortune"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, p. 305 quotes: "league, I believe you are more believing in the Bible than I. I am not, and, he added, with the expression of great inner emotion, you are much happier than I. I must say that so often in earlier times when I saw people of the lower classes, simple manual laborers who could believe so rightly with their hearts, I always envied them, and now, he continued, with soft voice and that naive childlike manner peculiar to him, while a tear came to his eye, tell me how does one begin this?..."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Bessel-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Bessel_48-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bessel never had a university education.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, p. 300.</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153_33-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981153_33-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBühler1981">Bühler 1981</a>, p. 153.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Gerhard_Falk_1995_121-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gerhard_Falk_1995_121_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGerhard_Falk1995">Gerhard Falk (1995). <span class="cs1-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/americanjudaismi00falk" rel="nofollow"><i>American Judaism in Transition: The Secularization of a Religious Community</i></a></span>. University Press of America. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/americanjudaismi00falk/page/121" rel="nofollow">121</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7618-0016-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7618-0016-3"><bdi>978-0-7618-0016-3</bdi></a>. <q>Gauss told his friend Rudolf Wagner, a professor of biology at Gottingen University, that he did not fully believe in the Bible but that he had meditated a great deal on the future of the human soul and speculated on the possibility of the soul being reincarnated on another planet. Evidently, Gauss was a Deist with a good deal of skepticism concerning religion but incorporating a great deal of philosophical interest in the Big Questions, that is. the immortality of the soul, the afterlife and the meaning of man's existence.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=American+Judaism+in+Transition%3A+The+Secularization+of+a+Religious+Community&amp;rft.pages=121&amp;rft.pub=University+Press+of+America&amp;rft.date=1995&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7618-0016-3&amp;rft.au=Gerhard+Falk&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Famericanjudaismi00falk&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACarl+Friedrich+Gauss"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBühler1981152-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBühler1981152_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBühler1981">Bühler 1981</a>, p. 152: "Closely related to Gauss's political and social views were his religious beliefs. Despite his religious beliefs. Despite his strong roots in the Enlightenment, Gauss was not an atheist, rather a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a> with very unorthodox convictions, unorthodox even if measured against the very liberal persuasions of the contemporary Protestant church."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004356-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004356_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, p. 356: "I must confess that such old theologians and song writers as Paul Gerhard have always made a great impression on me; a song by Paul Gerhard always exerted a wonderful power on me, much more than, for example, Moses, against whom as a man of God I have all sorts of qualms."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004305-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004305_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDunnington2004">Dunnington 2004</a>, p. 305.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMorris_Kline1982">Morris Kline (1982). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RNwnUL33epsC&amp;pg=PA73" rel="nofollow"><i>Mathematics: The Loss of Certainty</i></a>. Oxford University Press. p. 73. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-503085-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-503085-3"><bdi>978-0-19-503085-3</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Mathematics%3A+The+Loss+of+Certainty&amp;rft.pages=73&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1982&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-503085-3&amp;rft.au=Morris+Kline&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRNwnUL33epsC%26pg%3DPA73&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACarl+Friedrich+Gauss"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.encyclopedia.com/topic/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.aspx" rel="nofollow">"Gauss, Carl Friedrich"</a>. Complete Dictionary of Scientific Biography. 2008<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">29 July</span> 2012</span>. <q>In seeming contradiction, his religious and philosophical views leaned toward those of his political opponents. He was an uncompromising believer in the priority of empiricism in science. He did not adhere to the views of Kant, Hegel and other idealist philosophers of the day. He was not a churchman and kept his religious views to himself. Moral rectitude and the advancement of scientific knowledge were his avowed principles.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Gauss%2C+Carl+Friedrich&amp;rft.pub=Complete+Dictionary+of+Scientific+Biography&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.encyclopedia.com%2Ftopic%2FCarl_Friedrich_Gauss.aspx&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACarl+Friedrich+Gauss"></span></span>
</li>

</li>
